RATING: 7.5
I picked this up at the Dice Tower Con virtual flea market. I could see my rating going up with more plays, especially if/when I pick up a few expansions. The card decks for each character reminds me of Smash Up (not mixing and matching 2 decks, but having one deck that you won't go all the way through), and the card play is reminiscent of Star Realms. It might be a tinny bit longer than it needs to be, but it does feel like an epic battle with the villain. I still have not come close to defeating any of the standard difficulty 3 villains.

RATING: 6
There is a whole lot going on here.  It's a co-op, not really a deckbuilder, not really hand management or even tableau management.  It's really coordinated action management.  It's challenging and incredibly interesting, I'm just not sure if it's fun (a whole lot of accounting has to happen as you play).

I like it, the puzzle of dealing with the villain each turn is very similar to the challenge of Mage Knight. I've only played it solo (running 3 and 4 characters), but believe it would be much more fun with more players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
If ever there was a love hate game then this it.  Criticisms first.  The game doesn't scale well and words best with four players.  For 2 players I'd suggest choosing another 2 heroes and flipping them over to use their defeated power once per turn, i.e. they become your supporting side kick. This works so amazing well, and makes thematic sense, that I have to criticise the game designers for not suggesting this in the rules.  It's just lazy.  Speaking of the rules... oh the rules... 

The rules are horrible.  They are not clear and the text on the cards, although accurate, is not intuitive.  Most experienced card game designers try their very best to reduce the amount of text on game cards.  Sentinels actually added more text in the 2nd edition.  The solution was, rather than add more text, to clarify and make a better rule book, add some well chosen card symbols for common actions, and only use short unambiguous card text. This is unfortunate because underneath there's a fantastic co-op game.  

This is one of the few co-op games where a dictator is less of a problem.  There are so many cards and so much going on, it's almost impossible for one person to keep track of everything.  This means that players can make their own decisions without fear of reprisal or coercion.  It also 'feels' like a superhero game.  I don't beat a villian by having a card with a higher number, I use my superpower and hit for 3 lightning damage.

RATING: 5
I cant quite figure out this game. This was probably the 2nd or 3rd game I grabbed when I got into board gaming. We have played it two or three times. Tonight was probably the 3rd time with the last time being close to 8 years ago. 

I am really struggling to put words to this. I think its too random to be a deep strategy game. I think its too independent to be a good co-op game. I feel that a players move lacks any real depth. And then add just enough vagueness that a game that should take less than an hour to run through takes almost two because every time a new card comes up its 5 minutes of reading and discussing the results.

We tried and just could not get going. I think my max damage was 6dmg and I am not sure I could have done much more than that. Honestly if I would have played different cards it would have not made a difference because the environment or the boss draw would destroy them. Like I said I am struggling to figure out my true issue with this game and just cannot. I think overall it will end up being traded or thrown up on ebay. Its just not for us.

RATING: 10
What a great game with a real Superhero - Comicbook feel.
The rules are simple, there is plenty of re-playability, and finally it is not a "who has the most money to buy every Expansion-pack for Deckbuilding" game.

One starts with a fixed number of Hero-Cards, draws new Cards every round, plays a power and tries - in Co-op with his fellow teammates - to build powerful "Power-ups" to damage the evil Villain and prevent his evil plans. 

Really worth the money. My son (8) loves this game.

RATING: 9
Pros
- Superhero decks and they all play differently
- Villains with varying difficulties and themes
- The cards make thematic sense for the damage they do and how they do it
- Tells a cool story of a battle
- Simple structure of playing a card, use power, draw card
- Even though there's a lot of text at times, friends can ask for help since it's a co-op so it all evens out
- Glossary in the manual to clarify certain words
- Expansions add more variety and no more complexity

Cons
- It might get math-y at points
- A little fiddly, but the pieces and markers keeps that in check

The best? The game has a lot of replayability and, if you like the superheroe theme and want some game to play while chatting it's great. The base game has ten different superheroes to play, four locations and four different villains, enought to play a few games without having the same deck nor enemy.

And there are a lot of expansions in case it gets stale for you and your group.

RATING: 6
Cool fully cooperative card game. I like the quick setup and the easy rules. The game is very thematic and fun to play. Each player simply selects a hero and grabs his deck of cards. We then select a bad guy and a location to fight him at. The artwork is nice and there is a lot of replayability. 

UPDATE: I lowered the rating because I don't like the fact that the difficulty of the game is randomly set depending on the group of heroes and villain you choose at the start of the game. RATING: 4
Rating based on one play.

All the different hero, villian, and environment decks were really interesting.  I wanted to to explore all of them.

But the game itself was just not very fun.  The basic gameflow rules are simple and clean, but there are lots of questions about how cards interact.  The biggest problem though is that the table fills up with cards and it's just a slog to remember and process them all.

And it mostly ended in a few incapacitated players waiting while the final hero(s) chipped a few HP away from the villian, and the villian chipped a few HP away from the hero(s) in a race to zero.  We decided to quit earyl instead of playing it out.

The only reason I could be convinced to try it again would be to explore a new hero deck, but I expect that I'd end up equally bored.

RATING: 10
After searching far and wide for a good super-hero game this little gem appeared from nowhere and quickly filled that niche. Highly evocative of comics and appropriately thematic. All heroes and villains have their unique style and feel and the game is very good at wrapping it up together in team-based play. Goes much smoother with one or two designated players at the table assigned to tracking states, otherwise it can get overwhelming and mathy. Replayability is very high, with multiple environments, villains, heroes and even advanced modes of play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Fun card game where a group of heroes faces off against one of several evil masterminds, of varying difficulty levels. Each hero has a unique deck of cards which they play to make attacks or give themselves or other players buffs, whilst the evil mastermind brings out strong attacks, missions, and flunkies to protect them.

Easy to pick up, quite quick to play, and the artwork is quite cool. I would say that the heroes aren't as well balanced as they could be, and certain combinations of them work much better than others. The higher difficulty evil masterminds are also almost tortuous to fight, especially if you haven't chosen a hero whose deck counters effectively. Still, it's fun and engaging, and good for the occasional play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
This game introduces an original superhero universe where players attempt to defeat villains and overcome treacherous locations on their way to victory. The basic gameplay is simple and the barrier of entry is low, but once the game revs up there is a lot of book-keeping to manage and (with this enhanced edition) a lot of tokens hanging around your cards. The artwork is vibrant, though relatively weak (in my opinion) and combined with the simplicity of the ruleset the game can feel a bit homemade. Had it taken place in a more well-established universe like Marvel or DC, the game would likely have a more epic feel, but if you're willing to buy into a new universe that doesn't take itself too seriously, you should enjoy the game well enough - it's critically acclaimed and generally well-regarded for a reason.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I like this game a whole lot while despising it at the same time.  A love hate thing.

Very fiddly coop game that requires a lot of book keeping.  Easy to overlook some effect or to make a mistake while playing.  Fun experience for a coop superhero game.  Each character (villain, hero, environment) does play and feel different which gives the game replay value.

RATING: 7
I love super heroes and I love cooperative games, so I knew I was going to like this game. You'll have to forgive me if any of my opinions on it are a little off, though, because we played with some expansion elements mixed in and I was unaware of what came from what. I liked that you were required to plan where you would direct your attacks, and then there was a slow build as your characters gear up and become more dangerous. It did seem a little complex dealing with all the villain's stuff, and the environment stuff. At least one player has to be very fluent in the game to manage all that. However for the other players it can be straightforward, as they simply play a single card per turn. You could feel a little bit of the theme, but I could have used more genuine teamwork. Taking turns swinging at the enemy seems odd, and limits the amount of genuine cooperation. Obviously if it was licensed with real comic book heroes it would be one notch better, but it was easy enough to correlate these heroes with licensed characters. If the game was slightly more streamlined I'd probably want to own it, but with all the fiddly little rules to manage I doubt it would see enough play with my family.

RATING: 7
KNOCKING IT DOWN FROM 8 to 7 because:
a) I suspect it will be too easy
b) I suspect if I were running the villain deck, I would enjoy it less.

I guess one concern might be, with a 75% ratio - is the game too easy?  Granted, we only fought level 3 villains, and no level 4s.

Might very well pick this up rather than the new Cyclades expansion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
This is a great example of a game that does nothing new but does everything right. The rules are simple, but allow a lot of space for meaningful decisions. There are heaps of card interactions between the various decks. The decks are dripping in theme, and the theme is married to the mechanics really well.

It's a superb example of what can be accomplished with CCG-like card interactions in a non-deckbuilding co-op. Scratches the same itch as LotR, but without the frustrations of building a deck for that game.

Its single biggest flaw is that it doesn't scale well at all - in fact, it makes no effort to scale. Most villain and environment damage seems to hit all players simultaneously, but a significant enough proportion of damage targets highest or lowest HP player, which means in smaller games the same characters will take damage more often. It wouldn't have been too hard to make both damage output and hit points scale per number of players.

EDIT: I'm trying to figure out how a team of relative no-names could have produced something this robust in such an under-the-radar fashion.

After 15 games - often three or more in a row because it's so short an so much fun - I'm staggered by how robust and internally coherent the ruleset is. Unlike most CCGs there are no complicated timing rules, and no exceptions for 'balance' - and yet the whole edifice is really well-balanced and contains surprising depth.

About the only thing I don't like is the number of cards that destroy equipment cards - there are three heroes that rely on equipment (ie are useless without it) - Wraith, Bunker, Absolute Zero - and they are disproportionately targeted by anti-equipment cards. Most of these allow you to take damage to save your equipment, but one or two simply eradicate your equipment, and with it all the turns of building up AND all your options for doing things on your next turn. Not so much fun.

I am now in a bit of a trap with this game - I can see it being fun pretty much forever. It's already killed off any interest I have in Lord of the Rings, and I think it might eventually get to the Race for the Galaxy level of plays, which is no mean feat. However, I don't want to rate it in relation to other games, because that throws the whole scale out. Still, it's a really great game on so many levels. Might eventually make a 9.

EDIT: Infernal Relics hit, and made the game just amazing. Bumped it to an 8.75.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game is just fun in a box. It essentially allows you to play a CCG/LCG with three of your buddies together. Because the combos you make with your own cards and those of others help the team, you also get a high-five from the other player(s) rather than a crushed look. The fact that the game is able to incorporate ten different hero decks and allow a three to five player CCG with such streamlined rules is a thing of beauty (it's surprising that no one has done it successfully before). Some of the hero decks succeed particularly well in feeling like the hero they are supposed to represent. That and the whimsical art develops the theme and invites you to get into the spirit of the game. There are some criticisms that should be addressed. The game doesn't come with HP trackers or a mechanism to keep track of modifiers that do play a large role in the game. However, downloadable HP trackers are available and, with a few ten sided dice, the problem is solved. With regard to value, you are getting almost 600 cards for under $30. Some also have mentioned that the game doesn't scale well. This depends. The villains do vary substantially in difficulty and this information can be found on BGG. Some villains will play better with fewer or more heroes. However, the game is just as easily and enjoyably played with players controlling more than one hero (really). I enjoy SotM solitaire quite a bit for this reason, playing four heroes most of the time. If you like co-op games, CCG's, thematic games and the super hero theme, this one needs to be in your collection.

Update: The guys at >Games are nothing if not responsive to the game's fans. All of the criticisms listed above have been addressed in the "Enhanced Edition," which replaces the original. A fun game just got even more fun.

Update: Downgraded to a still solid seven (though I don't expect it ever to go lower). I sometimes feel like the game is "chippy," whittling away at numbers on the villain and hero cards - and the theme starts to slide off the experience. I love the idea and love the designers and still definitely like the game though I think there are more satisfying, dynamic co-op games out there.

RATING: 8
This actually belongs to my 7 yo son - a birthday gift from his grandparents (my suggestion).  He loves it.  I think it is a game that tells a good story, but is somewhat lacking in meaningful decisions - the best play is often obvious, and when it is not, the options are usually equivalent.  

But my son loves it.  He loves teaming up with me instead of competing against me.  He loves being a superhero, loves doing damage, loves that we keep winning convincingly.  This is a great, great introduction to boardgaming for him, and and great activity for us to do together.  His 5 yo brother also likes to help run one of my heroes, so this is wonderful quality time with both of them.  

I've rated SoM an 8 - this is specifically for playing with my kids.  It is a wonderful way to spend time with them.  If I were playing with adults, it probably drops to a 7 - I think I would still be willing to play, but I'd rather play something else.  The game does a good job of being an interesting experience, but after 5 plays I feel like there are not many interesting decisions - most decisions are driven by what cards you have, and they are relatively straightforward.  Sometimes, you must make a difficult choice.  In these cases, you are typically choosing blind between two options.  The benefit of your choice is then determined by what cards are subsequently drawn.  It is fairly unsatisfying from a thinky, puzzle-solving point of view.  In this respect it is not nearly as interesting as other co-ops such as Pandemic or Flash Point.  But as a story-telling mechanism it excels.  As a way of letting a child be a superhero with his Dad, it is a fantastic use of an afternoon.

RATING: 7
I'm normally not at all a fan of games like this, where there are a bunch of cards with a bunch of text, all doing special powers that combo off from one another.  However, after giving this one a few plays, the chore of tracking all of this became much more second nature, and the fun of the game really shines through.

The replayability here is very high, as the various heroes work together in interesting and numerous ways, and the effectiveness of each hero against the various bad guys can vary a ton as well!  It's really interesting to see the match-ups, and how things play out from game to game.

Each hero, bad guy, and environment also has very different and well through out themes, making it really fun to experience each game.  Overall, just a fun game of good guys trying to beat down bad guys, with a ton of variability to keep it fresh game after game.

RATING: 9
This is one of the best cooperative games I've played. Each hero deck has vastly different playstyles and strategies. The villains and environments add replay value and varied levels of difficulty. The components are fine; I lean towards games that incorporate stuff like boards, dice, and miniatures though, and this is just a card game (although the fact that I rated it a 7 based on gameplay alone means that I feel VERY highly about the gameplay). I cant stress enough how well the coop works; our group always makes sure to deliberate on which abilities we are going to use, which enemies to attack, and which overall strategy we'll use to win. The art is good (not great, a little amateurish but I have a soft spot for it because it looks a lot like the comics I used to draw in high school) and consistent. You can tell the creator spent a lot of time creating the different art for all the cards and that he really had a vision for the overall theme of the game.

RATING: 4
I read lots of comics. I'm constantly looking through the Eisner award winners for something to read (as a student, comics have become my go to for leisure reading).

Sentinels of the Multiverse plays like a comic, if comics were nothing but one long massive interminable fight. Each turn feels like a panel in a comic book if each panel showed every single punch kick laser blast whatever whether or not it had any impact whatsoever. And to win the fight, superheroes had to swing hundreds of punches.

Add to it, that many of the villains the heroes are facing, bring back threats you've already defeated, so that you can read the exact same panels you read five minutes ago all over again!

Heck, even when you are facing a new threat, you are reading the same comic panel all over again (a punch, a kick, a laser blast, whatever) except the target has changed.

I hereby give this game the award for game that least understands why comic books are successful as a medium.

This game also wins an award for first game to make me wish for player elimination. When you die, you do not get to leave the table of a game that might go on for another hour after you've died (recall, punch after painstaking punch, regardless of whether or not it has any impact). No, instead you have to stick around and use an ability each turn. And often that ability is just to let another player take an action. So you say, hey, does anyone want another action? Someone says yes, you say, ok go for it. And that's your turn. That's why they keep you at the table for another hour.

On the plus side, the game certainly passes on a sense of teamwork in that you feel like individuals moving towards a common goal as opposed to one hive mind doing all actions collectively. The biggest reason being, moves are usually really obvious, deal the most damage you can to the biggest threat unless someone else says they can handle it (in which case go after the second biggest threat). And the second biggest reason is that there is so much text, it is so small, that you have no idea what the capacities of the player across the table is. There is no way you could tell help them figure out what to do, so you just trust them to make the right call instead (of course, not having any idea what they are doing has me simply zoning out when it isn't my turn).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game has low replay value, unless you invest in promo cards and expansions.  But it's still a really good time!

RATING: 7
Let's start with a rating of 7. I picked up SotM with the hope that I'd be able to play this solo as much as with a group. After one play solo and one with a group, I'm pretty pleased so far.

The game does an excellent job of conveying the feel of a superhero team against the big villain. It's a great marriage of theme and mechanics. Although I haven't played the various superhero deckbuilder games, I suspect I'd strongly prefer this setup, where each hero has their own deck. You identify more with your role and thematically it makes more sense.

There's certainly a fiddly element to the game, tracking damage buffs and other variables, but I'm guessing with familiarity that becomes easier. The Enhanced edition of the game is very attractive, and very functional, and you get the sense that a lot of tender loving care went into the publishing of this game.

Update: Eventually sold it off. It's just a bit too fiddly for my preference. I'd happily play it, but I don't need to own it.

RATING: 8
An awesome cooperative game that is modular enough to be different every time and offer whatever level of challenge the player is in the mood for. The theme of superheroes is also awesome, and the art and flavor texts do an excellent job of creating a new comic book multiverse. 
Occasionally the game may feel like it plays itself or that what you should do is obvious (happens more or less depending on the complexity of your hero). Also, some villains may be more annoying than challenging; it can be especially frustrating when a villain card manages to completely demolish your hero's set up by eliminating all ongoing or equipment cards, severely limiting what your hero can even do. 
Overall though, it's a great game! It may just require an occasional break if you've been bringing it out to the table a lot lately.

RATING: 7
Pros

Sentinels has a simple mechanic that is easy to teach new players and is generally very fun to play.

the theme is fairly well done & the game has a lot of replayability.

the different characters give you a different experience each time you play it and many build up over time which is generally quite enjoyable.

there's a good amount of player interaction & cooperation and the alpha dog issue here seems less than a lot of other co-op games.

the game plays quickly enough and is fun along the way such that losing games are still quite fun.

Cons

there's a lot of variety in villains & heroes but there are also combinations that can easily lead to the game seeming quite unbalanced. I've played several sessions where we randomly hit on a very powerful hero combo and stomped the villain without any feeling of danger along the way. I've had the opposite happen as well. That said each game play isn't long enough that you mind too much and most sessions leave everyone involved feeling like they had a lot of fun.

support characters are very useful in the game but are less fun for most personalities to play as what you're doing is making others really powerful where you might feel like you're not doing too much. this isn't really a design problem necessarily but it's something to be aware of.

as simple as the rules are it's very easy to forget about one of a villain or henchman's abilities as lots of abilities modify other abilities.

RATING: 8
A card game, but not the type you're probably used to: it's neither a Magic nor a Dominion. Each superhero is represented by a deck which has a different strategy, and you work together to fight against a boss and the environment. This sounds great (actually it is pretty great) but you quickly realize that each hero can only do a small selection of things. If you're the Flash, would you like to run around and do a little damage to everyone...or run around faster and do a little less damage to a few more people? That's the sort of thing you're going to notice pretty quickly. Their version of the Flash does small but area damage, their Batman takes out single big guys, ect. That's not bad, and there's a fair amount of replay if you get the expansions, but once you beat each villain once or twice you probably won't feel the need to play it anymore. If you're looking for more meat, the Marvel Legendary game supposedly surpasses Sentinels in most ways.

RATING: 10
Look, I know the game is fiddly.  Well man/woman up and deal with it!  With Great Power comes Great Fiddliness!  And the fiddliness can absolutely be mitigated by buying an additional $5 app.  And the lack of variety in the base box can be mitigated by buying one or two $20 expansions.  So what?  They're like gadgets, and you're like Batman, and you gotta catch them all...

Alright, Sentinels takes a bit work to love.  Until you learn the heroes and villains and environments, you're going to have some terrible games.  But! Just crank some 80's tunes and think of the first few games as a training montage and eventually, after dropping many eggs, you'll successfully carry it in a spoon across the finish line with a freeze frame high five accompanying the final power chord.

I also recommend playing two players with two heroes each.  Double fisting heroes makes it more fun!

RATING: 5
This game can be really fun, and has a lot of good things about it: the rules are simple enough for lighter gamers to quickly dive in; each hero deck has a notably different feel to it; the theme's exciting and well-done; the number of different heroes / villains / environments is excellent for a base game. And while it's not exactly a deeply strategic game, when you've got a good matchup, the decisions feel meaningful and the tradeoffs can be nail-biting.

But... making that good matchup is the tricky bit. There is *very* little guidance about what makes a good game, and the claim that 3 or 5 both play about the same as 4 is something of an exaggeration. Even an immensely oversimplified system (eg: "Each hero can handle roughly 10 Difficulty. [Villain X] = 30 Difficulty, +5-10 if the heroes have no "damage all enemies".) would be better than nothing. Matchups which are too easy are really dull; games which are too hard are more interesting, but can be frustrating if they're too much too hard.

(I've heard that the Rook City expansion has some villains which scale better to the number of players, which appeals.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Components: Great realization of the theme. The artwork is simplistic, but hits all the right notes with the action shots and the names of cards. I also like that the cards sometimes feature other heroes in them.

Coming with ten heroes, four villains, and four environments allows for great replayability right out of the box. Every game with playable characters should come with this much content.

The graphic design of the cards, however, is quite poor. The font is cartoonish and small; too small to read unless right in front of me. Important information is buried in long sentences.

The rules leave a LOT to be desired. Again, important information is buried in unlabeled paragraphs. Character bios are sprinkled throughout when they would have been better served pushed to the back (like the building bios in Blue Moon City). We've had constant questions about basic rules of the game, specific card powers, timing, card interactions, etc. There are already 200+ threads in the rules forum here on the Geek, which is too many to sift through for a game that isn't delivering enough from the get go.

Experience: With 2 players, this game just doesn't work. We've been stomped by Baron Blade and by Citizen Dawn, and both times it seemed impossible that we could have won. The advanced rules mention playing with two heroes per player (with 2 players), but there are already so many cards to keep track of, I'm not thrilled at the idea of having even more information to track. This game doesn't seem smart enough to scale to the number of players (more players = easier).

A quick rant about cooperatives: the more information a cooperative game layers on the âvillainâ side, the more likely the players will forget this information. As in other games, each player scrutinizes their own information, and interprets rules to their advantage, and always remembers to use their own powers. But there's noone giving such scrutiny to the opposition, and we have little incentive (even if not consciously) to remember to enact each villainous action. In this recent game of Sentinels of the Multiverse, Citizen Dawn had 10+ villain cards out at once, and we had to march through them all each round, applying each of their effects when appropriate. Felt like work.

First Played: 2012-05-27
Last Played: 2013-03-12
Eagerness to Play Again: Eventually

Plays (3):
2 players: 3

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.6
Very nice co-operative card game. As a comic book reader and collector for almost my entire life, I'm a sucker for the theme. I really appreciate how each hero, villain, and environment plays differently and thematically. Although many people seem to love the artwork, I find it somewhat amateurish (sorry) compared to modern comic book artwork. I appreciate that Greater Than Games came up with their own comic book characters and mythology, but I wish I was playing with familiar DC Comics characters instead.

Some combinations of heroes and villains and locations just don't play well together, unfortunately, and those setups should be avoided. Learning what those bad combinations are, however, is a process of trial and error.

I have found that the gameplay gets boring with repeated plays. It's really about the narrative. You need to use your imagination to make the gameplay interesting. It's also very fiddly with the constant upkeep of hit points and damage buffs and debuffs. I find that aspect of the game draining and not fun. The few combos that each hero has are slow to materialize as you are typically only playing 1 card and drawing 1 card each turn. It's like playing in slow motion. There's also often little choice in what you should do.

I recently purchased the iOS app to play on my iPad. It's superior to the physical game in that you don't have to keep track of hit points and any damage buffs/debuffs. That speeds up the gameplay considerably and makes it far more enjoyable.

RATING: 4
Maybe I'm biased because the first game I played against the Matriarch and Anubis, but this game involves a lot of calculating minutiae like: deal X damage to the non-environmental target with the 2nd-highest HP; also deal Y + Z damage to the non-hero target with the 2nd lowest HP; etc.  There is often an optimum card play out there, but you have to do mental arithmetic to find it.

And that's the biggest problem: most decisions are not all that interesting and you have all the downtime in the world to maximize.

In its favor, it's very simple to teach.

RATING: 10
Sentinels of the Multiverse is a card driven cooperative game that is rich in theme and easy to play. This game provides boatloads of replayability and is very easy to take out and play. The concept of 1 villain deck + 1 environment deck + 3-5 hero decks is extremely modular, easy to expand and creates rich, different scenarios each game. The difficulty of this game is a big swingy but I think it actually adds to the theme because you can never know just how dangerous the supervillain will be at any given time. Overall I find Sentinels of the Multiverse to be a really fun thematic experience. It is not only one of my favorite solo games but also my favorite cooperative game of all time.

Creates an excellent narrative just about every time you play. Can get bogged down slightly with upkeep but the game is just too much fun to pass up. The Heroes and Villains feel very tangible and real, and all of them play uniquely. I like the art too! 

I mostly play solo with a 3 Hero team although my wife joins me for a game occasionally.

I use the following houserules:
Ambuscade: Trap cards activate as they come up in the Villain deck, and are then reshuffled back into the deck.
Chokepoint: On her flipped side, increase damage dealt and reduce damage taken by X, where X is the number of cards face-down in the villain play area from that particular deck.
Spite: Does not heal on damage, just destroying victims.

RATING: 10
I love cooperative games, and Sentinels is the best of the non-realtime ones (because trying to compare this to Space Alert is unfair to both games).  The game is all about narrative: the struggle to take down the Big Bad before all is lost.  Even without the expansions (and trust me, once you get hooked you WILL want all the expansions), there is so much replay value right out of the box that it will take many many plays before anything will start to seem like old hat.  The mechanics are simple, but the strategies as a team are really the key to this game... as such, I know of no other cooperative game (again, not counting Space Alert) that works so hard to create such variability and yet promote teamwork more.  An excellent design.

RATING: N/A
This game captures its theme better than any other superhero game I have played.  The art fills me with joy.  Every hero and villain has his/her own distinct feel, conveyed not just by flavor text, but by the mechanisms of their individual decks.  The downside is that despite the rather simple rules framework, there is a LOT to keep track of.

The app version of the game is also wonderful.

The bad news is that my plays of the app have convinced me of two things:
1) I will never get anyone to play this with me
2) Even if I did, there is pretty much no chance I will play the game correctly.

Consequently, I think I will rely upon the app for my Sentinels fix, and pass on my cards to someone with a brain big enough to keep track of the game state.  The Sidekick app is helpful, but is also an admission of sorts that there is just way too much bookkeeping in this game.  I would love it if GtG eventually comes out with a revised edition that keeps the interesting aspects but streamlines the need for information tracking and the reduces the complications of applying effects.

RATING: 5
First, the artwork in this game is outstanding.  They perfectly captured the look and feel of comic book heros and villans.   It's weird that the Environments are text only, however.   There are some pretty sloppy design elements:  ambiguous text and powers, no scaling for number of players, balance issues, and highly variable degrees of difficulty.   All of these things can be managed up front by experienced players, but you'd think the design could have been polished up a bit more.   There are a lot of +1s and -1s to deal with, but it isn't hard.   Gameplay is quite simple, too:  play a card, choose and use a power.   Try to defeat the villain (not a player) before you die.   But, despite all that, the art and setting make it still work.   Casual and quick, don't look for anything beyond that.

RATING: 10
Wow, this is just a darn good game with very few flaws.  I enjoy playing it more than Legendary, which WAS my favorite card game until I bought this.  The only one thing that Legendary has that Sentinels is missing, is the scheme cards.  Each time you fight a villain in Sentinels, they have the same deck and the same conditions for victory.  In Legendary, you have a villain AND a scheme, which adds more variety and playability.  Hopefully, this is something they may consider in future expansions.  The heros and villains are all pretty cool and mostly balanced, and even though this is game is unlicensed (another thing that favors Legendary), it really doesn't take much imagination to figure out what Sentinel corresponds to what Marvel/DC superhero(s).  The only small complaint I have is when you first start playing it, it has a very steep learning curve, much like Dominion.  You are going to be overwhelmed with all the complex text on the cards and keeping track of all the ongoing powers and such.  Basically, expect to screw up a lot, and expect to be beaten by the bad guys alot as well.  Each villain card has an advanced mode that makes them harder (which you probably won't be using in your first 25 play throughs), but I almost wish they would have included a novice mode, for younger and inexperienced gamers to get a feel for the game without completely being decimated.  Of course, everyone is free to house rule those, but the BIG caveat to house ruling Sentinels is that the game has a very delicate balance, one that you can really screw up if you start changing the rules.

RATING: 7.5
This is a cooperative card-based game in which players choose from a multitude of Heroes and battle a chosen Villain in an Environment.  Each Hero, Villain, and Environment have their own individual card decks, so this is not a drafting or deck-building game.  

Every Hero's own decks are nicely designed, leading them to play quite differently.  The same goes for the Villains and Environments.  You can tell that a lot went into the designs for each deck, as each card has it's own graphic and flavor text.  

Common complaints with the game include the bookkeeping that is necessary and some ambiguity with rules.  There is a good amount of reading to do on each card and you also need to keep track of the different effects that are chained together.  This is definitely fiddly and a hassle, which leads to almost no mistake-free games.    

That being said, this game is still fun and engaging.  The individualized card decks give players the feeling that they are playing a unique superhero, each with their own strengths and weaknesses, depending on the Villain they're up against.  I also really enjoy the different Environments as they can not only hurt the players, but can actually be very beneficial if managed/manipulated correctly.  

The amount of Hero, Villain, and Environment decks available, coupled with all of the expansions available, ensure that no 2 games will play the same!  Recommended!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Sentinels of the Multiverse is a card game co-op with comic book/super hero theme. Upon playing it, I immediately got a video game vibe from it. First of all, you have a team of characters that are fighting a boss which will usually spawn lesser enemies and can have devastating special attacks. In addition, there are many damage types (fire, lightning, energy, melee, projectile, etc.) in the game which can be boosted, reduced, redirected and rendered useless through immunity. One of the 'bosses' has the video game boss cliche of when it's dealt a type of damage it becomes immune to that type, so you have to keep switching damage types against it. To me, it almost felt like an MMO in card format where you have a team which will try to take down a boss and each team member serves a role, be it control, dps, healing, and tank. There are other games which have tried to mimic such a thing such as WoW: TCG with raid decks and there are Magic: The Gathering sets which also had one player as a boss and others playing the heroes, trying to beat the boss. I never played these specific modules, but I do know about each of these games and they didn't mimic the MMO feel the way Sentinels does.

One of the great things about this card game co-op over the previously mentioned CCGs and even the LotR LCG is that this is a much cheaper option. Sentinels does have expansions, but they aren't released monthly and you know what you're getting when you buy it because all expansions are the same so it doesn't feel like a financial burden. In addition, I feel you get a lot for your money. Consider Yomi's complete set and 16 factions for Summoner Wars... both would cost you $100+ and only give you about the same amount of cards as Sentinels of the Multiverse which costs about $35 - $40 and the prices for the original Sentinels is probably a bit cheaper than the Enhanced Edition. I would even consider the replay value of Sentinels to be comparable with Summoner Wars (16 factions) and Yomi Complete Edition as Sentinels comes with 10 Heroes, 4 Villains, and 4 Environments. You use 3 - 5 Heroes, 1 Villain and 1 Environment per game. That's a lot of configurations and really a stunning value for your money.

The Enhanced Edition add tokens to remind players of various status effects, damage tokens which was sorely needed, and a bigger box to put everything.  Beyond that, the rulebook is better and the added artwork to all of the environment cards which makes the game feel a bit more consistent. I can't help but mention the oversized villain cards. They aren't included in the box but were introduced with the a Enhanced Edition as a kickstarter stretch goal but is also available as a separate purchase. These are a great edition and should just be included in the game as the oversized cards make the bosses seem more menacing. It's definitely the definitive version of the game you want to buy and upgrade to if you are the owner of the original edition. 

That said, this game does have some negative aspects: the game is very text heavy and you will need to re-read cards often (mostly the villain cards).  Beyond that, you will be moving around a lot of hp tokens and tracking tokens that I mentioned earlier.  Finally, a lot of villain and environments will attack people based on their HP, so, you're constantly having to figure out who has the highest or lowest or second highest and second lowest.  So, if you really can't stand bookkeeping this game may bother you.  Even digitally, this game just isn't as fun as I found it to be the first time. If you do like this sort of game, it's a great value. This game is not for me though.

Aeon's End fires this game, in my opinion.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
There are very few games that make me want to replay them just as soon as I finish one playthrough. Sentinels of the Multiverse Enhanced Edition is one of those games I could pick up and play over and over and OVER AGAIN!  The theme is simple, you are a part of a team of superheroes and there is a villain rampaging through the streets, base, city, etc and it is your job to stop them.  This game while 2-5 players really can be soloed easily as you just have to play the role of the other players, but becomes more fun when you and a group of friends breathe a sigh of relief when you finally take down the villain. With 10 heroes, 4 villains, and 4 environments in the base game, you are hard-pressed not to pick this one up as inexpensive as it is!

RATING: 4
Superhero co-op card game. Great idea! 

But the execution ... poor. I think if you're someone who likes just playing cards for "cool effects", that's fine but otherwise, the game has no narrative. It's got the "what" and "how" but the "why" is sadly lacking. This lack of a narrative also means it falls down badly as a co-op. Co-ops should be about interaction, i.e., players actually working together and cooperating. Just saying all the players are against some AI with no thought as to how the players can work together does not make a co-op game.

Usability - also poor. Gameplay involves various cards on the table. All these cards interact one way or the other. So you've got to read the card text, which is tiny, on all these cards laid out on the table. Just painful.

RATING: 5.5
In evaluating this game, there are three versions you have to consider: 1) the board version without the phone app, 2) the board version with the phone app, and 3) the PC/iOS version. I play the PC version (Steam) and enjoy it as a diversion. I can tell from my experience on Steam that I wouldnât want to invest in the physical copy and play the game that way for the reason that this game is more aptly titled, âModified Modifiers: the Card Game.â 

What I mean is, the game is one of dealing and receiving damage that is usually modified by other effects in play, and keeping track of all those sounds like it would seriously detract from my enjoyment of the game. The PC version handles all that so I can just focus on playing my cards. The phone app supposedly helps in this regard, and maybe it does, but Iâm nowhere near motivated to find out when I have the convenience of the PC version to enjoy. 
 
So, thatâs my preference for how I play the game; now I can comment on the game play itself.  I enjoy the game for what it is â choosing different hero decks that complement each other and creating effective card combos. Choosing the different hero decks is no small matter here. Whereas in LOTR TCG you build your deck, here you build your team by choosing a prebuilt hero deck. Some characters are better at dealing damage, others at healing, and others at removing annoying ongoing effects. You need to consider both the villain youâre playing and the environment.

In a clever (albeit often annoying twist), you have to deal with both the villain youâre fighting as well as the physical locale where the battle is taking place. You might be in dinosaur land or a metropolis. The environment lashes out at both heroes and villains (though mostly heroes), so youâre often fighting a two-fronted war. The villains all have a special ability like flipping to a different side or mid-game healing or pulling things out of their discard, etc. 

The fun point of the game, then, is choosing the right hero combination then playing cards effectively. Each hero has a distinct way of playing that you need to learn in order to set them up for an effective combo tableau. Sometimes I just set up a game, go in and get wiped out because I donât know what to expect or I donât know the heroes Iâm using, so I reset with a different team and approach. In this regard, itâs similar to playing an adventure in LOTR TCG where you then tweak your deck. For people that donât like to deckbuild with individual cards, here you look at the different hero decks and choose.

The downside of the game is that itâs often âAttrition: the Card Gameâ and you play round after round of basically the same thing as you whittle the villain down from dozens or hundreds of hit points until theyâre dead. There are some combinations that are either impossible or unlikely to win, and it can be frustrating.

RATING: 7
[u]The Good[/u]: 1) Captures the essence of a group of Superheroes facing off against a mastermind Villain. 2) Huge amounts of replayability combined with the fact that it can be played solo. 3) Tons of high quality fan-made content that expands the game even further.

[u]The Bad[/u]: Laden with heaps of math and book keeping. [u]The Bottomline[/u]: If you enjoy the classic comic book clashes between Superhero and Supervillain, and you don't mind a bit (ton) of book keeping, this game will deliver big.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
While I've never been a big comic book reader, I certainly do appreciate the genre and its tropes. And of course, who hasn't fantasized about being a superhero at one time or another? Luckily, Sentinels of the Multiverse does a great job of making the player feel like a superhero in an action-packed comic book.

Sentinels of the Multiverse makes great use of its card-driven mechanics. Each hero in the game has his/her own (fixed) deck, and they all operate slightly differently from each other, allowing for a variety of gameplay styles. The villain and environment decks make sure each game is different, even when using the same decks. What's amazing is so much variety is possible even though everything follows the same, basic, easy-to-follow set of rules. The downside, which is shared with many other card-driven games, is that the rule interactions do occasionally get complicated and ambiguous. Furthermore, it can sometimes be difficult to keep track of all the ongoing effects in play, although there are markers to somewhat alleviate that. Another consequence of using decks to drive the gameplay is that luck can play a huge role in determining whether games become too easy or too hard. That said, Sentinels of the Multiverse plays so smoothly using its mechanics that I would almost always be willing to put up with the drawbacks to save the world once again.

RATING: 5.5
Pros
-Superhero theme, cooperative game
-Has mashup of different bosses and locations
-Decent number of heroes, they play somewhat differently but limited by the game mechanics (see cons below)
-Fairly easy to teach
-Flavor text is nice, rulebook is comic book format/fun, art is good quality (personally not a huge fan of the style but I can acknowledge its competently done at least).

Cons
-Very one dimensional. Everything in the game focuses on damage. If the ability doesn't do damage, it gives +damage to another effect or gives you -damage for a turn.
-Scales fairly poorly with number of players (only some abilities refer to number of players)
-Very little decision making. Each turn, draw one card, play one card and activate one ability. The choices typically are obvious (I play my turn in about 2-4 seconds)
-One card a turn no combo potential
-Different attack elements for the sake of just having different types (and there is a lot of them), they don't have any inherent differences.
-The heroes mostly play the same way

RATING: 7
Sentinels of the Multiverse makes me feel like a super hero. The theme just oozes out of this game. It really works for me.

Unfortunately, the game suffers from being a bit too mathy and fiddly. The way the various heroes, villains and environments all interact is a huge selling point and helps keep the game interesting, but it can quickly become difficult to track everything. My group almost always gets something wrong.

Fortunately, there's a digital version of the game found on Steam that handles all the fiddliness for you. It doesn't have the full collection of heroes, villains and environments available yet, but it drastically speeds up the game while keeping track of all the details, making it my preferred way of playing the game.

My recommendation is to pick up a copy (or two, to play with a friend) of the digital version first to determine if this is a game you're interested in learning. If you love it (and really want the tactile feel of playing the physical version), then pick up the original and its many expansions.

RATING: 8.15
Great game, but the unpredictable play time means that it doesn't hit the table terribly often. However, still very fun and addictive.

The enhanced version is far superior to the original version in terms of bits. The storage in the box is great and the HP chits and modifiers are essential. Supposedly they balanced some cards as well, although I didn't play the original enough to notice a difference.

I agree with most of the complaints about this game (fiddliness, luck of the draw), but I think it makes up for its shortcomings. With the counters the fiddliness is reduced to a reasonable level. Depending on the characters and how many people are playing there can be a lot of tough decisions on your turn.

If you're looking for deeper strategy, Legendary may be a better bet. If you're looking for light strategy in a completely coop environment, Sentinels is better.

RATING: 7
After about 20 plays or so. I still enjoy this one, but the flaws of the game are starting to wear on the plays a bit and the progression on challenge has been recently falling a but short.

Sentinels is a fairly easy game to learn and very fun on the first few plays. The amount of calculations can be overwhelming at first, but easily manageable with the iPhone app. Learning how each deck (character) works against weaker opponents is great. Discovering epic synergies between characters is a blast and using them to take down some of the harder villains is very satisfying and achieves a successful overall cooperative experience. Sentinels is not without its flaws, however.

The major issue is the degree of challenge. The higher difficulty villains have felt more punishing rather that challenging. A game design issue which is very difficult to deal with in coop games since the AI is simply a deck of cards, but important to establish to get a well-flowing game.  There has been too many situations where we were stuck in impossible situations, by bad draws, and ongoing effects by cards. Challenge is good, but being stuck in overwhelming situations in the FIRST round limits strategies and decision making. The players feeling hopeless and overwhelmed from the get go. 

Still, Sentinels is a blast, especially when players work together to defeat harder villains. Discovering that solid combo for your character or working up to do massive amounts of damage is awesome, a good enough reason to play despite its flaws.

RATING: 9

RATING: 8
Excellent game, overflowing with theme.  Plays great solitaire and the variety with all the expansions is amazing.  I love the asymmetry with all the characters and villains.  I also love how this game is all about using your superhero powers directly to affect the enemy rather than some vague card-building mechanic that doesn't make nearly as much sense.  Its like having some awesome prebuild decks to play magic the gathering with!

I love the advance game option to make it really tough.  Brilliant, will invest more in the game as more of it comes out. 

The new version does make me a little upset because I now have to get rid of the old version, but the new (H) stat really scales the game beautifully for any number of players and makes it all worthwhile.  I also recommend getting the extra-large villain cards, they are brilliant and add more theme to the game (totally optional of course).  I got these with some Sentinels of the Multiverse T-shirts and the promo villain and hero that I ordered from Greater than games.  The T-shirts are AWESOME!

RATING: 9
If you want to be a superhero, this is the game for you. You just feel like the hero you are playing. The decks are fixed, so you do not "build" before or during the game. Just pick you hero(s), villain, and environment and start playing. 
Some combinations of heroes, villain, and environment can result in lots of ongoing effects and some tedious tracking. Keep yourself organized and it's not bad. 
There is an app to help with all the tracking, but I have not used it, and I refuse to sully my tabletop experience with the video game version.

RATING: N/A
I was introduced to Sentinels of the Multiverse by William Bell (the same weekend that I first learned 7 Wonders) and I really enjoyed it. I was still in my honeymoon period with Magic: The Gathering and the combo card play reminded me of my favorite aspects of MTG. 

So I came home from that weekend and purchased the first copy of the game I found (it was out of print at the time); this was the first cooperative game I'd ever played and the first I introduced to my family.

My wife didn't really care for it, although she would play from time to time. My sons however took a shine to the game and played it quite a bit on their own. Unfortunately for me, this meant they rarely felt like playing it when I wanted to.

Eventually I decided to trade it out of the collection--I'm not that interested in playing coops any more, and it didn't seem likely this was going to get played often enough to keep on the shelf.

RATING: 5
Sentinels is a card game akin to Magic: the Gathering.  It is not collectible, but players each have their own unique deck comprised of dozens of cards with various abilities and powers.  As the game progresses, players will usually have multiple cards in front of them, each expanding or supplementing their powers, enhancing the powers of their teammates, or even hindering the power of the villain and his minions.  The ultimate goal is to take down the villain before he achieves his victory conditions, which are different for each adversary.

A word about the artwork:  it is excellent.  Adam Rebottaro is the artist, and if he is not already an artist for a comic book publisher, he should be.  He captures the look and feel of comic book superheroes.  In a nice touch, each card also contains a small blurb of banter that also evokes an appropriate comic book atmosphere.  In another pleasant surprise, the cards are completely devoid of those troublesome icons.  Each card contains all the text needed to understand its use â¦ and this is kept brief.  So, players don't have to remember the meaning of a mind-numbing avalanche of icons.

My biggest complaint against the game â and this is a deal-breaker for me â is its very nature.  As mentioned, it is somewhat akin to Magic: The Gathering, a game I've never played but based on my observations probably would not enjoy.  As more and more cards are played, there is a dramatic increase in the number of effects and modifiers that will apply to just about every subsequent card that is played.  This means that just about every time a card is played, every other card in play must be consulted to see whether or not it modifies the effect of the just-played card.  This is tedious, time consuming, calculating and, well, dull.  Many folks don't mind this process, but I find it sucks the very life out of a game.  Sadly, for me, it is a game killer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 1.5
Extremely dull cooperative game, with no interesting decisions and no sense of narrative. You just draw a card, play the best card, activate the best power (which in my case was the only power during the whole game), ad nauseum. The only thing in its favor is the superhero theme. I admit I'm not a fan of this type of game, with lots of different cards with text that can be in effect at the same time, but the biggest problem is that most effects are totally boring: deal damage, increase damage, reduce damage and draw cards. By the second round, I just wanted the game to end.

RATING: 7
Having made some effort into addressing the key defects of the first printing (HP tracking, balancing for player count) and some unessential upgrades (making a great-looking game even prettier), this is easily recommended. The best comic book game I've ever played, and also the very best fake comics canon I've ever seen.
[EDIT] I've had to downgrade my rating. I still love the game, but sometimes the "meta" of a given session is overly harsh on some characters. This isn't problematic from a difficulty perspective (it's a co-op, who cares), but it is extremely troublesome from a fun perspective--if your hero happens to be useless against a certain villain, the game will suck for you.

RATING: 7.25
Sentinels of the Multiverse takes a deck management game, with an original set of superheroes and does a pretty fair job of recreating the comic book days of pulp glory.
Each character (hero & villain) has their own deck, as do the different environments where hero/villain encounters take place.
From simple play rules, the various powers in the decks seem to play out nicely, challenging the heroes to stop the villains before the heroes are defeated or the villain's nefarious plans succeed. 
The design is simple, yet the play is not, and heroes strengths and weaknesses play into winning or losing.
At a start I'm ranking this a 7.25 but can easily see that increasing if it proves as addictive as it is rumored to be.

RATING: 6.7
It is a co-op; all the players are trying to defeat the big bad guy in an environment, both which are chosen from many options at setup. Each player chooses a character from many options, getting their personal deck of cards.

The cards have a ton of text on them--twice as much as Smash Up.
This is one of those games where it would be good to know generally how your character should function, so repeat plays are kind of required. There is also a lot of basic math flying around--lots of adding from this card effect, subtracting from that card effect. Not quite as crazy as Suburbia's growing tableau of effects, but similar. Easy to miss things.

So not my cup of tea.
That being said, I would maybe be willing to play this game one more time because the particular setup we did was absolutely dismal for my chosen character. 
I chose Fanatic, who has many cards based around hindering the bad guys' minions. Only we were fighting Plague Rat, whom had no minions appear. If I were familiar with my character, it would have become "keep drawing cards and hope you draw the card that does a ton of damage when you're nearly dead." Eh.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
When I see this game on my self, I want to play it. When I play it, I want to put it back on the shelf.

The theme of this game is SO appealing! I love the concept of how each player picks a different hero deck and together we work towards overthrowing the villain. Pretty straight forward right? But what makes this so appealing is that the superheroes are all new and original. Sure there are elements that are CLEARLY borrowed from other more well known heroes, but not so tactless were it's just a reskinning.

But why this gets such a low rating from me is because of the fiddliness of the game. There are constant +1, -1 damage modifiers happening. What type of damage being done is also constantly changing. 

It seems so hard to get good combos to happen with your cards. Some heroes do not work well with others. So this requires heavy familiarity with the game from the git-go.

The whole experience leaves me feeling like there's something missing from the game.

RATING: 4.5
Game I liked at first, and really wanted to like.  In fact, to begin with I liked it better as a superhero game than any of the deckbuilders with that theme.  However, I tired of it quickly.

Sentinels works really well on the easier levels with easier bosses and is a fun game.  However, it becomes tedious when you ramp up the difficulty of the villains.  The actual damage dealt per action by most heroes is quite low and the villains have high HP.  In addition, the more difficult heroes and villains have tons of static effects that are all in play later in game, making it necessary for there to be tokens just to keep track of all the effects, and making the turns take forever because everything you do involves multiple calculations to see what effects happen and how much damage is done to whom.

In general, the game involves a great deal of one step forward two steps back.  Between that and all the unnecessary fiddliness of all the actions, it starts to feel like cooperative Agricola with superheroes.  I play a lot of Euros, and if I want to sit down for 2 hours APing moves and calculating the 3rd step ramifications of each play, I have a whole shelf of cube pushers I can turn to.

RATING: 6
A game that does a wonderful job at realizing its theme (superheroes fighting against agents of evil and corruption), but is just too damn fiddly for its own good a lot of the time. 

I will describe two polar opposite experiences one can have even within a single game of Sentinels:

There are moments that, when the various decks interact with each other and within their own design, the gameplay just takes on a wonderful flow, and the cards and counters and underlying mechanisms melt away and one truly feels like a part of a super-powered team trying to save the world against overwhelming odds. 

And then there are moments when the game screeches to an abrupt halt as players struggle to keep track of all the various damage modifiers, conditions, and card effects and counter-effects such that the only heroic feeling comes from having actually managed to fully complete a turn feeling like you didn't make any errors or oversights (which, let's face it... you still messed up somewhere along the line). 

This makes for an incredibly erratic game experience. When it works? The game is great and a lot of fun. When it doesn't? It's about as tedious as it gets. I'm finding myself wanting to play less and less as the prospect of trying to keep track of everything just makes the prospect of playing the game not as appealing when I can play other games that give way more back for the same amount of effort.

RATING: 5
First off, I am not the target audience for this so take that into consideration with my comments and such, was never into comics or even Superheroes all that much. Having said that, that was something that bugged me right off the hop, the fact the heroes were generic nobody's (apparently Legendary does that part right) 

The game was very standard fare, and nothing notable really stood out about it for me other than co-operating 'seemed' important so hopefully teamwork is rewarded (well any co-op failing to do that is a failure though...). Personally I think its more suited to kids artwork and theme-wise and [i]almost[/i] game-wise, but it seems I am in the minority here maybe thinking this and note comic books fans should be happy with the artwork I believe. Thing is too I said almost game-wise as, well, decisions are very light, but there is a lot of text everywhere and things to keep track of (even a PITA for adults), so younger kids likely couldn't handle it that well. I'm guessing here maybe 8-10 years and up, and to me I think it makes a great family game where you would play it with your kids and have a good time doing so.  

I think for the target audience its a good co-op, and a fairly thematic one, you are a team fighting against the bad guys, however theme could have been stronger too as its missing narrative/story arc, characters are generic and some other things could have made it feel more thematic as well. Card stock seemed good in version I played, but lots of duplicate cards in the decks and some things were not balanced well either. Due to theme and the very standard gameplay where there just isn't that many interesting decisions (try to do as much damage as you can, maybe heal if you can, the only interesting part is just the discussions) there just isn't anything to really draw me back to it personally. Maybe the intro scenario is that much more lame than the others not sure but even if so I really didn't see any reason to come back to it. If it played in 30 or 40 mins tops my rating would likely be a point or two higher, 1 hour or especially more than that to me is too long for what if offers.

RATING: 8.5
A great co-op card game.  Lots of variety with all the different heroes you can play as, each with their own unique deck and play style.  Lots of villains to fight, again each with their own deck and mechanics.

The balancing mechanic for more or less players doesn't always work particularly well.  Some seem more tuned to more or less players, making them exceedingly difficult if you're playing with the "wrong" number of players.

It's not a breezy card game.  The cards often have a lot of text, and some cards have multiple effects that trigger at different times during a turn, sometimes forcing you to read several cards multiple times in a single turn.

But overall an extremely fun game with the right group that encourages communication and team work.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

The mechanics of Sentinels of the Multiverse can be so fidgety game as to potentially ruin the game, but the theme is so strong here I can deal with it. It really does feel like the players are a team of superheroes using their unique abilities to thwart a villain. That, combined with good number of heroes, villains, and environments give the game excellent replay value.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Pros: 
To me, the theme of this game is its strength.  The Superhero world it creates satisfies my desire to play some of my favorite Superheroes (Like Batman/Wraith and Flash/Tachyon) while introducing some really unique and cool characters as well (like The Argent Adept and Mr. Fixer).  While the world Greater than Games has created is amazing, no doubt, I feel the ultimate thematic success of this game lies within how well the mechanics of each deck works so well with each superhero's abilities.

There are a number of other things I like about this game.  I love the art in this game.  I love the co-op nature of the game.  And I love the diversity of characters and mechanics in the game.

Con:  I have one complaint about this game, and unfortunately, its the game play itself.  My only complaint about this game is that unless you are playing one of the more complex characters, the decks feel to me like they pretty much play themselves.  I know people talk about how hardcore gamers and non-gamers alike can participate in this game, but I feel like save for a couple heroes (like Absolute Zero) you are pretty much hoping to draw "that one card" and simply flipping through a deck of cards praying you don't die.

An awesomely thematic game, it is just not fun to me.  Overall, a good game but not a great game.

An Alternate Suggestion: BattleCON: Devastation of Indines

RATING: 10
Is there a way to give this an 11! This game has it on every level. If your a comic fan the characters have great flavor, and are thinly veiled versions of heros you love. The wraith feels like you're Batman, beating up thugs with gadgets and martial arts. Legacy protects people and inspires them like superman, and the villans each feel completely different. That's probably the coolest thing about the game. In the core set, Grand Warlord Voss plays like a young Thanos taking over a world with his army of mercenaries where as Barron Blade feels like a base you're attacking to stop his super death ray he's built in a lab. The mechanic for each villan makes it feel like a completely different game- which is fantastic. I love the nods to the comics as well. Visionary has a card with art that's her using her physic powers to mind wipe a villain, and it's a homage to the art from x-men 25 where Xavier wipes Magneto. It shows that the makers know and care about the material. Finally what makes it fun is the difficulty. It's really hard. We lost the first three games we played against baron blade with two people and we had a blast doing it. Iron Legacy from the new expansion is even worse, and the one time we beat him we didn't even play him using one of his mechanics. So when you win together you feel like you really earned it! The only complaint I see is that I do feel like the game could scale a little bit better. The cards have an X=the number of players, mechanic so they're harder with more people,  but if you do play with 5 people, I do feel like you roll the villain pretty easily. Id say 3 is the magic number, 2 is fun and REALLY hard, and 4 is on the easy side with some villains, but still great. Overall Greater Then Games has really hit the nail on the head, and I look forward to there "firefly" style game they've announced. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Initial Rating: 7.0 (February 2013)

What a good game. Iâm pleasantly surprised :D. After playing Legendary (and hearing the negative comments of the DC comics one), I didnât want to play more super hero games with decks in it, however I really like this game. Sentinels of the Multiverse has a huge potential to rise higher in my ratings, after all I love card games.

The game has some interesting decisions, but it is not very hard to play. What I mean, is that gameplay is not very deep, but it is FUN.

Its main strength is the theme, the outstanding art and the easy mechanics.  Also the play time is around 1 hour. 

The components are adequate, but the cards begin to degrade pretty quickly, they need to be sleeved ASAP. I really feel this is a 2 or (preferably) 3 player game, with 4 I begin to feel the downtime.

My main concern is the game has a low replay value as my main interest would be to kill each villain, also as the game is not deep, I fear it would bore me pretty soon. For now I want to play again and I will comment on my finds. 

Current Rating: 7.0

When we play again we'll look to make a team of heros that can each make some kind of active contribution.

RATING: 7.5
A nice light game with superheros.  It's got a feel that I used to get playing superhero rpgs.  It's excellent for that.  If you don't like superheroes, then ignore my score, cause you're probably not going to like this game purely on a mechanical basis.

I think the rulebook and some of the card descriptions are going to lead to confusion.  If you got a group that doesn't like to come up with house rules, there is going to be trouble.  For example, I'm still not sure what happens in the case of a tie for a target and there's sometimes where adding/reducing damage cards can be a bit confusing.

I also worry a bit some of the heroes might be a bit unbalanced.

I think tightening up some of the language on the cards and the rulebook in future versions of the game might help. Some sort of breaking up of the text of the cards to the phase they take affect in would have helped.  (So environment and villain cards could have had START: and END: in bold. 

Still, good fun and I can't think of anything on the market like it.

RATING: 9.5
So much constant interaction between players. Even when it's not your turn you're still involved. This is the way a cooperative game should be.

The listed time of 60 minutes seems unrealistic unless maybe if you've played the game dozens of times. After 4 plays it still takes 2-2.5 hours for a session. But just as it feels like it's starting to drag, it wraps up pretty quickly. The game can be pretty fiddly with lots of conditional actions to keep track of.

I've been surprised how often the game plays out like a superhero movie. Starts off slow, revealing the villain's plans gradually, then starts beating you down pretty hard. Then when all seems lost, the heroes make a miraculous comeback to win the day victorious. Or that doesn't happen and you are utterly destroyed. Either way it's always fun.

RATING: 2
To much fiddly rules to read on each card. The game is mildly interesting and the customizability is immense, but the game is one of the least elegant or fluid games I've ever played. 

Without a player present who has memorized every card, players are bound to miss a number of minor interactions and the time taken to read everything is more than it should be. It is not uncommon for a game to take 2+ hours to play.

Plus, there is a lot of downtime between actions and, like most coop games, the player's ability to win is dependent on random luck.

RATING: N/A
The biggest criticism of Sentinels is the extensive bookkeeping, and it's absolutely an issue. As the game progresses there can be a huge number of things to keep track of - things that happen at the start of a turn, things at the end, things triggered by things - this is pretty brutal if you're playing solo and trying to run several characters, but much less problematic if you're going multiplayer and everyone just has one hero to track. I don't recommend solo play until you've played enough that handling effects becomes second nature. That aside, bright and colorful, with lots of flavor, and you can really feel the different characters' identities, so there's a lot of fun to be had...as long as you're not too put off by all the tiny text you need to keep track of.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This game gets big props for its excellent art and character design, effectively creating a comic book mythos from scratch. This is a cooperative game that feels like you're playing out the contents of a comic book, with different heroes having strikingly different playstyles. With a group of friends, SOTM can be a tense and rewarding session, and the additional villains and heroes that come with expansions and available individually combine to make for a seemingly endless variety of villain/hero team combinations.

My biggest gripe with the game comes from the different ways the heroes play. My default way of picking characters is at random, especially with people that haven't played before. This can significantly amplify the difficulty, especially if a small group has a "support" character that isn't able to do much. Keep in mind that team construction matters a lot, and that the challenge of the game can be amplified considerably based on what heroes you have and what villain you fight, because the game is inherently unbalanced (which isn't a bad thing, but should be made aware of). Great game all around, highly recommended.

RATING: 7
A superhero co-op game that doesn't suffer from many of the problems of other co-op games. Each players takes on the role of a unique superhero in order to fight one of the many villains that exist in the game, in one of the many environments the game has to offer. Each deck of cards brings about its own different set of options. Players are free to help and aid in their comrades fight for a common cause, all the while doing so as they please. This game has tons of replay-ability with the expansions. Tons of superheros and tons of villains makes for endless combinations of fun.

RATING: 8.5
There is no big set up for the game and the rules are quite simple.
Heroes and villains decks are well balanced, each with its own mechanics.
Environment add a bit more tension to the game but can sometimes be used to the advantage of the heroes.

The only draw back is that the game is far more easier with four players.

The theme is really present with back stories given for all characters (heroes and villains) and deck mechanics tightly fitting those.

These makes it my favorite super-hero game for now.

Graphics are not of the highest quality but they have some consistency since all cards are drawn by the same artist and the characters are often featured on other character's cards.
Also the layout of the cards and the use of the tokens give the game the look of super hero comic books from the 60s.

Card quality is really great, the internal dividers and the tokens of the enhanced edition are very useful.

The most important thing you can do to make it fun is be sure to have a good facilitator sitting near the Villain/Environment. They need to be very familiar with the nuances of the rules, and willing to remember all the fiddly bits going on as the Villain tableau gets bigger and more complicated.
